<p><b>Maurice Harold Macmillan, 1st Earl of Stockton</b>, <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:100%;"><a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_Order_of_Merit" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of the Order of Merit">OM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">PC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Fellow of the Royal Society">FRS</a></span><sup id="cite_ref-frs_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-2">[2]</a></sup> (10 February 1894&#160;– 29 December 1986) was a British <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> politician and statesman who served as the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</a> from 10 January 1957 to 19 October 1963. Nicknamed "<a href="/wiki/Supermac_(cartoon)" title="Supermac (cartoon)">Supermac</a>", he was known for his <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatism</a>, wit and unflappability.</p>
<p>Macmillan served in the <a href="/wiki/Grenadier_Guards" title="Grenadier Guards">Grenadier Guards</a> during the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>. He was wounded three times, most severely in September 1916 during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a>. He spent the rest of the war in a military hospital unable to walk, and suffered pain and partial immobility for the rest of his life. After the war Macmillan joined his family business, then entered Parliament in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1924" title="United Kingdom general election, 1924">1924 General Election</a>, for the northern industrial constituency of <a href="/wiki/Stockton-on-Tees_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Stockton-on-Tees (UK Parliament constituency)">Stockton-on-Tees</a>. After losing his seat in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1929" title="United Kingdom general election, 1929">1929</a>, he regained it in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1931" title="United Kingdom general election, 1931">1931</a>, soon after which he spoke out against the high rate of unemployment in Stockton-On-Tees, and against <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a>.</p>
<p>Rising to high office during the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> as a protégé of wartime Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, Macmillan then served as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> under Churchill's successor <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Sir Anthony Eden</a>. When Eden resigned in 1957 after the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>, Macmillan succeeded him as Prime Minister.</p>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/One-nation_conservatism" title="One-nation conservatism">One Nation Tory</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Disraelian</a> tradition, haunted by memories of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, he believed in the <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">post-war settlement</a> and the necessity of a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">mixed economy</a>, championing a <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian</a> strategy of <a href="/wiki/Public_investment" class="mw-redirect" title="Public investment">public investment</a> to <a href="/wiki/Demand_management" title="Demand management">maintain demand</a> and pursuing <a href="/wiki/Tory_corporatism" title="Tory corporatism">corporatist</a> policies to develop the domestic market as the engine of growth. Benefiting from favourable international conditions,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiddleton1997422.E2.80.933_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiddleton1997422.E2.80.933-3">[3]</a></sup> he presided over an <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic history of Britain">age of affluence</a>, marked by low unemployment and high if uneven growth. In his <a href="/wiki/Bedford" title="Bedford">Bedford</a> speech in July 1957 he told the nation they had 'never had it so good',<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiddleton1997422_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiddleton1997422-4">[4]</a></sup> but warned of the dangers of inflation, summing up the fragile prosperity of the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Conservatives were re-elected in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1959" title="United Kingdom general election, 1959">1959</a> with an increased majority on an electioneering budget.</p>
<p>In international affairs, Macmillan rebuilt the <a href="/wiki/Special_Relationship_(US-UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Special Relationship (US-UK)">special relationship</a> with the United States from the wreckage of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> (of which he had been one of the architects), and redrew the world map by <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa" title="Decolonisation of Africa">decolonising</a> <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>. Reconfiguring the nation's defences to meet the realities of the nuclear age, he ended <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Conscription in the United Kingdom">National Service</a>, strengthened the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear forces</a> by acquiring <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris#British_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">Polaris</a>, and pioneered the <a href="/wiki/Partial_Test_Ban_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Partial Test Ban Treaty">Nuclear Test Ban</a> with the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. Belatedly recognising the dangers of strategic dependence, he sought a new role for Britain in Europe, but his unwillingness to disclose <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_States" title="Nuclear weapons and the United States">United States nuclear secrets</a> to France contributed to a French veto of the United Kingdom's entry into the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Near the end of his premiership, his government was rocked by the <a href="/wiki/John_Vassall" title="John Vassall">Vassall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Profumo_Affair" class="mw-redirect" title="Profumo Affair">Profumo</a> scandals, which seemed to symbolise for the <a href="/wiki/Anti-establishment" title="Anti-establishment">rebellious youth</a> of the 1960s the moral decay of the British establishment.<sup id="cite_ref-Leitch_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leitch-7">[7]</a></sup> After his resignation, Macmillan lived out a long retirement as an elder <a href="/wiki/Politician" title="Politician">statesman</a>. He was as trenchant a critic of his successors in his old age as he had been of his predecessors in his youth.</p>
<p>Macmillan was the last British prime minister born in the reign of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, the last to have served in the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>, the last to wear a moustache when in office, and the last to receive an hereditary peerage.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Historians.27_assessments_of_Macmillan.27s_premiership"><span class="tocnumber">5.6</span> <span class="toctext">Historians' assessments of Macmillan's premiership</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan was born at 52 <a href="/wiki/Cadogan_Place" title="Cadogan Place">Cadogan Place</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea, London</a>, to Maurice Crawford Macmillan (1853–1936), a publisher, and his wife, the former Helen (Nellie) Artie Tarleton Belles (1856–1937), an artist and socialite from <a href="/wiki/Spencer,_Indiana" title="Spencer, Indiana">Spencer, Indiana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher19822_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher19822-8">[8]</a></sup> He had two brothers, Daniel, eight years his senior, and Arthur, four years his senior.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne20089_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne20089-9">[9]</a></sup> His paternal grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_MacMillan" title="Daniel MacMillan">Daniel MacMillan</a> (1813–1857), who founded <a href="/wiki/Macmillan_Publishers" title="Macmillan Publishers">Macmillan publishing</a>, was the son of a Scottish <a href="/wiki/Croft_(Scotland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Croft (Scotland)">crofter</a> from <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Arran" title="Isle of Arran">Isle of Arran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schooling">Schooling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Schooling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan received an intensive early education, closely guided by his American mother. He learned French at home every morning from a succession of nursery maids, and exercised daily at Mr Macpherson's Gymnasium and Dancing Academy, around the corner from the family home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne200813_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne200813-11">[11]</a></sup> From the age of six or seven he received introductory lessons in classical Latin and Greek at <a href="/wiki/The_Eaton_House_Group_of_Schools#Eaton_House_Belgravia_Pre-Preparatory" title="The Eaton House Group of Schools">Mr Gladstone's day school</a>, close by in <a href="/wiki/Sloane_Square" title="Sloane Square">Sloane Square</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan attended <a href="/wiki/Summer_Fields_School" title="Summer Fields School">Summer Fields School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a> (1903–06). He was Third Scholar at <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton College</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._15_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._15-14">[14]</a></sup> but his time there (1906–10) was blighted by recurrent illness, starting with a near-fatal attack of pneumonia in his first <a href="/wiki/Half_(Eton)" class="mw-redirect" title="Half (Eton)">half</a>; he missed his final year after being invalided out,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne200816_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne200816-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and was taught at home by private tutors (1910–11), notably <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Knox" title="Ronald Knox">Ronald Knox</a>, who did much to instil his <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">High church</a> <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He won an exhibition to Balliol, but was less of a scholar than his older brother Dan.<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._15_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._15-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_and_early_political_views">University and early political views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: University and early political views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a child, teenager and later young man, he was an admirer of the policies and leadership of a succession of <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> Prime Ministers, starting with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a>, who came to power near the end of 1905 when Macmillan was only 11 years old, and then <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>, whom he later described as having "intellectual sincerity and moral nobility", and particularly of Asquith's successor, <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, whom he regarded as a "man of action", likely to accomplish his goals.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>He went up to <a href="/wiki/Balliol_College,_Oxford" title="Balliol College, Oxford">Balliol College, Oxford</a> (1912–14), where he joined many political societies. His political opinions at this stage were an eclectic mix of moderate Conservatism, moderate Liberalism and Fabian Socialism. He read avidly about <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Disraeli</a>, but was also particularly impressed by a speech by <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George,_1st_Earl_Lloyd-George_of_Dwyfor" class="mw-redirect" title="David Lloyd George, 1st Earl Lloyd-George of Dwyfor">Lloyd George</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Union_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford Union Society">Oxford Union Society</a> in 1913, where he had become a member and debater. He was a protégé of the then President <a href="/wiki/Walter_Monckton,_1st_Viscount_Monckton_of_Brenchley" title="Walter Monckton, 1st Viscount Monckton of Brenchley">Walter Monckton</a>, later a Cabinet colleague, and became Secretary then Junior Treasurer (elected unopposed in March 1914, then an unusual occurrence) of the Union and would, in his biographers' view, "almost certainly" have been <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_Oxford_Union" title="List of Presidents of the Oxford Union">President</a> had the war not intervened.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> He obtained a First in <i>Honours Moderations</i>, informally known as <i>Mods</i> (consisting of Latin and Greek, the first half of the four-year Oxford <i><a href="/wiki/Literae_Humaniores" title="Literae Humaniores">Literae Humaniores</a></i> course, informally known as <i>Greats</i>), in Hilary Term 1914. With his final exams over two years away, he enjoyed an idyllic Trinity (summer) term at Oxford, just before the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_One" class="mw-redirect" title="World War One">First World War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Supermac._Author_2010_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Supermac._Author_2010-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_service">War service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: War service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Volunteering immediately for active service in the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">Great War</a>, Macmillan joined the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and was <a href="/wiki/Officer_(armed_forces)" title="Officer (armed forces)">commissioned</a> as a temporary <a href="/wiki/Second_lieutenant" title="Second lieutenant">second lieutenant</a> in the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Royal_Rifle_Corps" title="King's Royal Rifle Corps">King's Royal Rifle Corps</a> on 19 November 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Promoted to lieutenant on 30 January 1915,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> he soon transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Grenadier_Guards" title="Grenadier Guards">Grenadier Guards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> He fought on the front lines in <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">France</a>, where the casualty rate was known to be high, as was the probability of an "early and violent death".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-18">[18]</a></sup> He served with distinction as a <a href="/wiki/Captain_(British_Army_and_Royal_Marines)" title="Captain (British Army and Royal Marines)">captain</a> and was wounded on three occasions. Shot in the right hand and receiving a glancing bullet wound to the head in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Loos" title="Battle of Loos">Battle of Loos</a> in September 1915, Macmillan was sent to Lennox Gardens in <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a> for hospital treatment, then joined a reserve battalion at <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Barracks" title="Chelsea Barracks">Chelsea Barracks</a> from January to March 1916, until his hand had healed. He then returned to the front lines in France. Leading an advance platoon in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flers%E2%80%93Courcelette" title="Battle of Flers–Courcelette">Battle of Flers–Courcelette</a> (part of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a>) in September 1916, he was severely wounded, and lay for ten hours in a <a href="/wiki/Slit_trench" class="mw-redirect" title="Slit trench">slit trench</a>, sometimes feigning death when Germans passed, and reading the classical playwright <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a> in the original Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The then-<a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">Asquith</a>'s own son, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Asquith" title="Raymond Asquith">Raymond Asquith</a>, was a brother officer in Macmillan's regiment, and was killed that month.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan spent the final two years of the war in hospital undergoing a long series of operations.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He was still on crutches on <a href="/wiki/Armistice_Day" title="Armistice Day">Armistice Day</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_11_November_1918" title="Armistice of 11 November 1918">11 November 1918</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> His hip wound took four years to heal completely, and he was left with a slight shuffle to his walk and a limp grip in his right hand from his previous wound, which affected his handwriting.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan saw himself as both a "gownsman" and a "swordsman" and would later display open contempt for other politicians (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>) who, often through no fault of their own, had not seen military service in either World War.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canadian_aide-de-campship">Canadian aide-de-campship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Canadian aide-de-campship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Of the 28 students who started at Balliol with Macmillan, only he and one other survived the war.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> As a result, he refused to return to <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a> to complete his degree, saying the university would never be the same;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne200849_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne200849-33">[33]</a></sup> in later years he joked that he had been "sent down by <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">the Kaiser</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Owing to the impending contraction of the Army after the war, a regular commission in the Grenadiers was out of the question.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> However, at the end of 1918 Macmillan joined the Guards Reserve Battalion at Chelsea Barracks for "light duties".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> On one occasion he had to command reliable troops in a nearby park as a unit of Guardsmen was briefly refusing to reembark for France, although the incident was resolved peacefully. The incident prompted an inquiry from the War Office as to whether the Guards Reserve Battalion "could be relied on".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan then served in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa, Ontario</a>, Canada, in 1919 as <a href="/wiki/Aide-de-camp" title="Aide-de-camp">ADC</a> to <a href="/wiki/Victor_Cavendish,_9th_Duke_of_Devonshire" title="Victor Cavendish, 9th Duke of Devonshire">Victor Cavendish, 9th Duke of Devonshire</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Governor_General_of_Canada" title="Governor General of Canada">Governor General of Canada</a>, and his future father-in-law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne200852_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne200852-38">[38]</a></sup> The engagement of Captain Macmillan to the Duke's daughter Lady Dorothy was announced on 7 January 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> He relinquished his commission on 1 April 1920.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> As was common for contemporary former officers, he continued to be known as 'Captain Macmillan' until the early 1930s and was listed as such in every General Election between 1923 and 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> As late as his North African posting of 1942–3 he reminded Churchill that he held the rank of captain in the Guards reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Publishing">Publishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Publishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On his return to London in 1920 he joined the family publishing firm <a href="/wiki/Macmillan_Publishers" title="Macmillan Publishers">Macmillan Publishers</a> as a junior partner, remaining with the company until his appointment to ministerial office in 1940. He resumed with the firm from 1945 to 1951 when the party was in opposition.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan married <a href="/wiki/Lady_Dorothy_Macmillan" title="Lady Dorothy Macmillan">Lady Dorothy Cavendish</a>, the daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Victor_Cavendish,_9th_Duke_of_Devonshire" title="Victor Cavendish, 9th Duke of Devonshire">9th Duke of Devonshire</a>, on 21 April 1920. Her great-uncle was <a href="/wiki/Spencer_Cavendish,_8th_Duke_of_Devonshire" title="Spencer Cavendish, 8th Duke of Devonshire">Spencer Cavendish, 8th Duke of Devonshire</a>, who was leader of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> in the 1870s, and a close colleague of <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>. Lady Dorothy was also descended from <a href="/wiki/William_Cavendish,_4th_Duke_of_Devonshire" title="William Cavendish, 4th Duke of Devonshire">William Cavendish, 4th Duke of Devonshire</a>, who served as Prime Minister from 1756 to 1757 in communion with <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pelham-Holles,_1st_Duke_of_Newcastle" title="Thomas Pelham-Holles, 1st Duke of Newcastle">Newcastle</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt,_1st_Earl_of_Chatham" title="William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham">Pitt the Elder</a>. Her nephew <a href="/wiki/William_Cavendish,_Marquess_of_Hartington" title="William Cavendish, Marquess of Hartington">William Cavendish, Marquess of Hartington</a> married <a href="/wiki/Kathleen_Cavendish,_Marchioness_of_Hartington" title="Kathleen Cavendish, Marchioness of Hartington">Kathleen</a>, a sister of <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>.</p>
<p>In 1929 Lady Dorothy began a lifelong affair with the Conservative politician <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boothby,_Baron_Boothby" title="Robert Boothby, Baron Boothby">Robert Boothby</a>, an arrangement that scandalised high society but remained unknown to the general public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe20102467.2C_2477_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe20102467.2C_2477-43">[43]</a></sup> Philip Frere, a partner in Frere Cholmely solicitors, urged Macmillan not to divorce his wife, which at that time would have been fatal to a public career even for the "innocent party". Macmillan and Lady Dorothy lived largely separate lives in private thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The stress caused by this may have contributed to Macmillan's nervous breakdown in 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> He was often treated with condescension by his aristocratic in-laws and was observed to be a sad and isolated figure at <a href="/wiki/Chatsworth_House" title="Chatsworth House">Chatsworth</a> in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Campbell suggests that Macmillan's humiliation was first a major cause of his odd and rebellious behaviour in the 1930s then, in subsequent decades, made him a harder and more ruthless politician than his rivals Eden and Butler.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Macmillans had four children:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maurice_Macmillan" title="Maurice Macmillan">Maurice Macmillan, Viscount Macmillan of Ovenden</a> (1921–1984)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lady_Caroline_Faber" title="Lady Caroline Faber">Lady Caroline Faber</a> (1923–2016)</li>
<li>Lady Catherine <a href="/wiki/Julian_Amery,_Baron_Amery_of_Lustleigh" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian Amery, Baron Amery of Lustleigh">Amery</a> (1926–1991)</li>
<li>Sarah Heath (1930–1970). A family rumour that Boothby was her natural father has been discounted by the most recent and detailed study.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe2010_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe2010-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Lady Dorothy died on 21 May 1966, aged 65, after 46 years of marriage.</p>
<p>Macmillan was in close friendship in old age with Ava Anderson, Viscountess Waverley, <i>née</i> Bodley (1896–1973), the widow of <a href="/wiki/John_Anderson,_1st_Viscount_Waverley" title="John Anderson, 1st Viscount Waverley">John Anderson, 1st Viscount Waverley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorpe201014116.E2.80.9314121_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorpe201014116.E2.80.9314121-49">[49]</a></sup> Eileen O'Casey, <i>née</i> Reynolds (1900–95), the actress wife of Irish dramatist <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_O%27Casey" title="Seán O'Casey">Seán O'Casey</a>, was another female friend, Macmillan publishing her husband's plays. Although she is said to have replaced Lady Dorothy in Macmillan's affections, there is disagreement over how intimate they became after the death of their respective spouses, and whether he proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Political_career,_1924–51"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career.2C_1924.E2.80.9351">Political career, 1924–51</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Political career, 1924–51">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Member_of_Parliament_(1924–29)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Parliament_.281924.E2.80.9329.29">Member of Parliament (1924–29)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Member of Parliament (1924–29)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan contested the depressed northern industrial constituency of <a href="/wiki/Stockton-on-Tees_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Stockton-on-Tees (UK Parliament constituency)">Stockton-on-Tees</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1923" title="United Kingdom general election, 1923">1923</a>. The campaign cost him about £200-£300 out of his own pocket.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The collapse in the Liberal vote let him win in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1924" title="United Kingdom general election, 1924">1924</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell_2009.2C_p._246_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell_2009.2C_p._246-55">[55]</a></sup> In 1927 four MPs, including Boothby and Macmillan, published a short book advocating radical measures.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell_2009.2C_p._246_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell_2009.2C_p._246-55">[55]</a></sup> In 1928 Macmillan was described by his political hero, and now Parliamentary colleague, David Lloyd George, as a "born rebel".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fisher_1982.2C_pp.32-3_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher_1982.2C_pp.32-3-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan lost his seat in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1929" title="United Kingdom general election, 1929">1929</a> in the face of high regional unemployment. He almost became Conservative candidate for the safe seat of <a href="/wiki/Hitchin_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Hitchin (UK Parliament constituency)">Hitchin</a> in 1931<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._243_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._243-57">[57]</a></sup> but the sitting MP, <a href="/wiki/Guy_Molesworth_Kindersley" title="Guy Molesworth Kindersley">Guy Molesworth Kindersley</a> cancelled his retirement plans, in part because of his own association with the anti-Baldwin rebels and his suspicion of Macmillan's sympathy for <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Mosley" title="Oswald Mosley">Oswald Mosley</a>'s promises of radical measures to reduce unemployment. Instead, the fortunate resignation of the new candidate at Stockton allowed Macmillan to be re-selected there, and he returned to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom House of Commons">House of Commons</a> for his old seat in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1931" title="United Kingdom general election, 1931">1931</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fisher_1982.2C_pp.32-3_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher_1982.2C_pp.32-3-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Member_of_Parliament_(1931–39)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Parliament_.281931.E2.80.9339.29">Member of Parliament (1931–39)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Member of Parliament (1931–39)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan spent the 1930s on the backbenches. In March 1932 he published "The State and Industry" (not to be confused with his earlier pamphlet "Industry and the State").<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._103_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._103-58">[58]</a></sup> In September 1932 he made his first visit to the USSR.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Macmillan also published "The Next Step". He advocated cheap money and <a href="/wiki/Economic_planning" title="Economic planning">state direction of investment</a>. In 1933 he was the sole author of "Reconstruction: A Plea for a National Unity". In 1935 he was one of 15 MPs to write "Planning for Employment". His next publication, "The Next Five Years", was overshadowed by Lloyd George's proposed "New Deal" in 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._103_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._103-58">[58]</a></sup> Macmillan Press also published the work of the economist <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell_2009.2C_p._246_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell_2009.2C_p._246-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan resigned the Conservative whip in protest at the lifting of sanctions on Italy after her conquest of Abyssinia. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Channon" title="Henry Channon">"Chips" Channon</a> described him as the "unprepossessing, bookish, eccentric member for Stockton-on-Tees" and recorded (8 July 1936) that he had been sent a "frigid note" by Conservative Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>. Baldwin later mentioned that he had survived by steering a middle course between Harold Macmillan and (the extreme right-winger) <a href="/wiki/John_Gretton,_1st_Baron_Gretton" title="John Gretton, 1st Baron Gretton">John Gretton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Next Five Years Group, to which Macmillan had belonged, was wound up in November 1937. His book <i>The Middle Way</i> appeared in June 1938, advocating a broadly centrist political philosophy both domestically and internationally. Macmillan took control of the magazine <i>New Outlook</i> and made sure it published political tracts rather than purely theoretical work.<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._103_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._103-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan supported Chamberlain's first flight for talks with <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a> at Berchtesgaden, but not his subsequent flights to Bad Godesberg and Munich. After <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich</a> he was looking for a "1931 in reverse", i.e. a Labour-dominated coalition in which some Conservatives would serve, the reverse of the Conservative-dominated coalition which had governed Britain since 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> He supported the independent candidate, <a href="/wiki/Sandie_Lindsay,_1st_Baron_Lindsay_of_Birker" title="Sandie Lindsay, 1st Baron Lindsay of Birker">Lindsay</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_by-election,_1938" title="Oxford by-election, 1938">Oxford by-election</a>. He wrote a pamphlet "The Price of Peace" calling for alliance between Britain, France and the USSR, but expecting Poland to make territorial "accommodation" to Germany (i.e. give up the <a href="/wiki/Danzig_corridor" class="mw-redirect" title="Danzig corridor">Danzig corridor</a>). In "Economic Aspects of Defence", early in 1939, he called for a Ministry of Supply.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Phoney_War_(1939–40)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Phoney_War_.281939.E2.80.9340.29">Phoney War (1939–40)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Phoney War (1939–40)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan visited Finland in February 1940, then the subject of great sympathy in Britain as it was being attacked by the USSR, then <a href="/wiki/Molotov-Ribbentrop_Pact" class="mw-redirect" title="Molotov-Ribbentrop Pact">loosely allied</a> to Nazi Germany. He wore a white fur hat, later to be seen on a trip to the USSR in the late 1950s. His last speech from the backbenches was to attack the government for not doing enough to help Finland. Britain was saved from a potentially embarrassing commitment when the <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a> ended in March 1940 (Finland would later <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Finland_during_World_War_II" title="Military history of Finland during World War II">fight on the German side</a> against the USSR).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan voted against the Government in the <a href="/wiki/Norway_Debate" title="Norway Debate">Norway Debate</a>, helping to bring down <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a> as Prime Minister, and tried to join in with <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Wedgwood,_1st_Baron_Wedgwood" title="Josiah Wedgwood, 1st Baron Wedgwood">Colonel Josiah Wedgwood</a> singing <i>"<a href="/wiki/Rule_Britannia" class="mw-redirect" title="Rule Britannia">Rule Britannia</a>"</i> in the House of Commons Chamber.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Parliamentary_Secretary,_Ministry_of_Supply_(1940–42)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliamentary_Secretary.2C_Ministry_of_Supply_.281940.E2.80.9342.29">Parliamentary Secretary, Ministry of Supply (1940–42)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Parliamentary Secretary, Ministry of Supply (1940–42)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan at last attained office by serving in the wartime coalition government as the <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Secretary" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary Secretary">Parliamentary Secretary</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Supply" title="Ministry of Supply">Ministry of Supply</a> from 1940. Channon commented (29 May 1940) that there was "some amusement over Harold Macmillan's so obvious enjoyment of his new position".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan's job was to provide armaments and other equipment to the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>. He travelled up and down the country to co-ordinate production, working with some success under <a href="/wiki/Lord_Beaverbrook" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Beaverbrook">Lord Beaverbrook</a> to increase the supply and quality of <a href="/wiki/Armoured_fighting_vehicle" title="Armoured fighting vehicle">armoured vehicles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher198278.E2.80.939_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher198278.E2.80.939-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Colonial_Under-Secretary_(1942)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_Under-Secretary_.281942.29">Colonial Under-Secretary (1942)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Colonial Under-Secretary (1942)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan was appointed <a href="/wiki/Under-Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Under-Secretary of State for the Colonies">Under-Secretary of State for the Colonies</a> in 1942, in his own words "leaving a madhouse to enter a mausoleum".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Though a junior minister he was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Privy Council">Privy Council</a>, and he spoke in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> for <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_the_Colonies" title="Secretary of State for the Colonies">Colonial Secretaries</a> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Guinness,_1st_Baron_Moyne" title="Walter Guinness, 1st Baron Moyne">Lord Moyne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_5th_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 5th Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Cranborne</a>. Macmillan was given responsibility for increasing colonial production and trade, and signalled the future policy direction when in June 1942 he declared:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The governing principle of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Colonial Empire</a> should be the principle of partnership between the various elements composing it. Out of partnership comes understanding and friendship. Within the fabric of the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> lies the future of the Colonial territories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher198282_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher198282-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Macmillan predicted that the Conservatives faced landslide defeat after the war, causing Channon to write (6 Sep 1944) of "the foolish prophecy of that nice ass Harold Macmillan". In October 1942 Harold Nicolson recorded Macmillan as predicting "extreme socialism" after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Macmillan nearly resigned when <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Stanley" title="Oliver Stanley">Oliver Stanley</a> was appointed Secretary of State in November 1942, as he would no longer be the spokesman in the Commons as he had been under Cranborne. <a href="/wiki/Brendan_Bracken" title="Brendan Bracken">Brendan Bracken</a> advised him not to quit.<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._151-60_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._151-60-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Minister_Resident_in_the_Mediterranean_(1942–45)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_Resident_in_the_Mediterranean_.281942.E2.80.9345.29">Minister Resident in the Mediterranean (1942–45)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Minister Resident in the Mediterranean (1942–45)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg/300px-Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg" width="300" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg/450px-Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg/600px-Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="463" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Allied_leaders_in_the_Sicilian_campaign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Macmillan (top row, left) with Allied military leaders in the Sicilian campaign, 1943; <a href="/wiki/Major_general_(United_States)" title="Major general (United States)">Maj-Gen</a> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bedell_Smith" title="Walter Bedell Smith">Bedell Smith</a> to his right. Front Row: General <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Eisenhower</a> (then Supreme Commander, Mediterranean), <a href="/wiki/Air_chief_marshal" title="Air chief marshal">Air Chief Marshal</a> <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Tedder,_1st_Baron_Tedder" title="Arthur Tedder, 1st Baron Tedder">Tedder</a>, <a href="/wiki/General_(United_Kingdom)" title="General (United Kingdom)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Harold_Alexander,_1st_Earl_Alexander_of_Tunis" title="Harold Alexander, 1st Earl Alexander of Tunis">Alexander</a>, <a href="/wiki/Admiral_(Royal_Navy)" title="Admiral (Royal Navy)">Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cunningham,_1st_Viscount_Cunningham_of_Hyndhope" title="Andrew Cunningham, 1st Viscount Cunningham of Hyndhope">Cunningham</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Harry_Crookshank,_1st_Viscount_Crookshank" class="mw-redirect" title="Harry Crookshank, 1st Viscount Crookshank">Harry Crookshank</a> had refused the job, Macmillan attained real power and <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a> rank late in 1942 as British Minister Resident at <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> in the Mediterranean, recently liberated in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Torch" title="Operation Torch">Operation Torch</a>. He reported directly to the Prime Minister instead of to the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Eden" title="Anthony Eden">Anthony Eden</a>. <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Lyttelton,_1st_Viscount_Chandos" title="Oliver Lyttelton, 1st Viscount Chandos">Oliver Lyttelton</a> had a similar job at Cairo, while <a href="/wiki/Robert_Daniel_Murphy" title="Robert Daniel Murphy">Robert Murphy</a> was Macmillan's US counterpart.<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._151-60_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._151-60-70">[70]</a></sup> Macmillan built a rapport with US General <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, then Supreme Allied Commander in the Mediterranean (SACMED), which proved helpful in his career,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2008158_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2008158-71">[71]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Crossman" title="Richard Crossman">Richard Crossman</a> later recalled that Macmillan's "Greeks in the Roman Empire" metaphor dated from this time (i.e. that as the USA replaced Britain as the world's leading power, British politicians and diplomats should aim to guide her in the same way that Greek slaves and freedmen had advised powerful Romans).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Casablanca_Conference" title="Casablanca Conference">Casablanca Conference</a> Macmillan helped to secure US acceptance, if not recognition, of the <a href="/wiki/Free_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French">Free French</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan was badly burned in a plane crash, trying to climb back into the plane to rescue a Frenchman. He had to have a plaster cast put on his face. In his delirium he imagined himself back in a Somme casualty clearing station and asked for a message to be passed to his mother, now dead.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Together with <a href="/wiki/Gladwyn_Jebb" title="Gladwyn Jebb">Gladwyn Jebb</a> he helped to negotiate the Italian armistice in August 1943, between the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Husky" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Husky">fall of Sicily</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Avalanche" title="Operation Avalanche">Salerno Landings</a>. This caused friction with Eden and the Foreign Office.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> He was based at <a href="/wiki/Caserta" title="Caserta">Caserta</a> for the rest of the war. He was appointed UK High Commissioner for the Advisory Council for Italy late in 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> He visited London in October 1943 and again clashed with Eden. Eden appointed <a href="/wiki/Duff_Cooper" title="Duff Cooper">Duff Cooper</a> as Ambassador to France (still under German occupation) and <a href="/wiki/Noel_Charles" title="Noel Charles">Noel Charles</a> as Ambassador to Italy to reduce Macmillan's influence.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In May 1944 Macmillan infuriated Eden by demanding an early peace treaty with Italy (at that time a pro-Allied regime under <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Badoglio" title="Pietro Badoglio">Badoglio</a> held some power in the southern, liberated, part of Italy), a move which Churchill favoured. In June 1944 he argued for a British-led thrust up the <a href="/wiki/Ljubljana_Gap" title="Ljubljana Gap">Ljubljana Gap</a> into Central Europe (Operation "Armpit") instead of the planned diversion of US and Free French forces to the South of France (<a href="/wiki/Operation_Dragoon" title="Operation Dragoon">Operation Dragoon</a>). This proposal impressed Churchill and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Alexander,_1st_Earl_Alexander_of_Tunis" title="Harold Alexander, 1st Earl Alexander of Tunis">General Alexander</a>, but did not meet with American approval. Eden sent out Robert Dixon to abolish the job of Resident Minister, there being then no job for Macmillan back in the UK, but he managed to prevent his job being abolished. Churchill visited Italy in August 1944. On 14 September 1944 Macmillan was appointed Chief Commissioner of the Allied Central Commission for Italy (in succession to General Macfarlane). He continued to be British Minister Resident at Allied Headquarters and British Political adviser to <a href="/wiki/Henry_Maitland_Wilson,_1st_Baron_Wilson" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Maitland Wilson, 1st Baron Wilson">"Jumbo" Wilson</a>, now Supreme Commander, Mediterranean. On 10 November 1944 he was appointed Acting President of the Allied Commission (the Supreme Commander being President).<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan visited Greece on 11 December 1944. As the Germans had withdrawn, British troops under <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Scobie" title="Ronald Scobie">General Scobie</a> had deployed to Athens, but there were concerns that the largely pro-communist Greek resistance, <a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Front_(Greece)" title="National Liberation Front (Greece)">EAM</a> and its military wing <a href="/wiki/Greek_People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="Greek People's Liberation Army">ELAS</a>, would take power (see <a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">Greek Civil War</a>) or come into conflict with British troops. Macmillan rode in a tank and was under sniper fire at the British Embassy. Despite the hostility of large sections of British and American opinion, who were sympathetic to the guerillas and hostile to what was seen as imperialist behaviour, he persuaded a reluctant Churchill, who visited Athens later in the month, to accept <a href="/wiki/Damaskinos_of_Athens" title="Damaskinos of Athens">Archbishop Damaskinos</a> as Regent on behalf of the exiled <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Greece" title="George II of Greece">King George</a>. A truce was negotiated in January 1945, enabling a pro-British regime to remain in power, as Churchill had demanded in the <a href="/wiki/Percentages_agreement" title="Percentages agreement">Percentages agreement</a> the previous autumn.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan was also the minister advising <a href="/wiki/Charles_Keightley" title="Charles Keightley">General Keightley</a> of <a href="/wiki/V_Corps_(United_Kingdom)" title="V Corps (United Kingdom)">V Corps</a>, the senior <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> commander in Austria responsible for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Keelhaul" title="Operation Keelhaul">Operation Keelhaul</a>, which included the forced repatriation of up to 70,000 prisoners of war to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a> in 1945. The deportations and Macmillan's involvement later became a source of controversy because of the harsh treatment meted out to <a href="/wiki/Collaboration_during_World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Collaboration during World War II">Nazi collaborators</a> and anti-partisans by the receiving countries, and because in the confusion V Corps went beyond the terms agreed at <a href="/wiki/Yalta_conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Yalta conference">Yalta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allied_Forces_Headquarters" class="mw-redirect" title="Allied Forces Headquarters">Allied Forces Headquarters</a> directives by repatriating 4000 <a href="/wiki/Repatriation_of_Cossacks_after_World_War_II" title="Repatriation of Cossacks after World War II">White Russian troops</a> and 11,000 civilian family members, who could not properly be regarded as Soviet citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2008251.E2.80.9386_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2008251.E2.80.9386-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Air_Secretary_(1945)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_Secretary_.281945.29">Air Secretary (1945)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Air Secretary (1945)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan toyed with an offer to succeed <a href="/wiki/Duff_Cooper" title="Duff Cooper">Duff Cooper</a> as MP for the safe Conservative seat of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_St_George%27s_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Westminster St George's (UK Parliament constituency)">Westminster St George's</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Horne_1988.2C_p._243_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horne_1988.2C_p._243-57">[57]</a></sup> Criticised locally for his long absence, he suggested that Lady Dorothy stand for Stockton in 1945, as she had been nursing the seat for five years. She was apparently willing. However, it was thought better for him to be seen to defend his seat, and Lord Beaverbrook had already spoken to Churchill to arrange that Macmillan be given another seat in the event of defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan returned to England after the European war, feeling himself 'almost a stranger at home'.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> He was <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Air" title="Secretary of State for Air">Secretary of State for Air</a> for two months in <a href="/wiki/Churchill_caretaker_ministry" title="Churchill caretaker ministry">Churchill's caretaker government</a>, 'much of which was taken up in electioneering', there being 'nothing much to be done in the way of forward planning'.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Opposition_(1945–51)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_.281945.E2.80.9351.29">Opposition (1945–51)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Opposition (1945–51)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan indeed lost Stockton in the landslide <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> victory of <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">1945</a>, but returned to Parliament in the November 1945 by-election in <a href="/wiki/Bromley_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Bromley (UK Parliament constituency)">Bromley</a>. In his diary <a href="/wiki/Harold_Nicolson" title="Harold Nicolson">Harold Nicolson</a> noted the feelings of the Tory backbenchers: "They feel that Winston is too old and Anthony (Eden) too weak. They want Harold Macmillan to lead them."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Macmillan played an important role in drafting the "<a href="/wiki/Industrial_Charter" title="Industrial Charter">Industrial Charter</a>" ("Crossbencher" in the <i>"<a href="/wiki/Sunday_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunday Express">Sunday Express</a>"</i> called it the second edition of <i>"The Middle Way"</i>) he now, as MP for a safe seat, adopted a somewhat more right-wing public persona, defending private enterprise and fiercely opposing the Labour government in the House of Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Political_career,_1951–57"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career.2C_1951.E2.80.9357">Political career, 1951–57</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Political career, 1951–57">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Housing_Minister_(1951–54)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing_Minister_.281951.E2.80.9354.29">Housing Minister (1951–54)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Housing Minister (1951–54)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the Conservative victory in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">1951</a> Macmillan became <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Housing_and_Local_Government" title="Ministry of Housing and Local Government">Minister of Housing</a> under Churchill, who entrusted him with fulfilling the pledge to build 300,000 houses per year (up from the previous target of 200,000 a year), made in response to a speech from the floor at the 1950 Party Conference. Macmillan thought at first that Housing, which ranked 13 out of 16 in the Cabinet list, was a poisoned chalice, writing in his diary (28 October 1951) that it was "not my cup of tea at all … I really haven't a clue how to set about the job". It meant obtaining scarce steel, cement and timber when the Treasury were trying to maximise exports and minimise imports.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> 'It is a gamble—it will make or mar your political career,' Churchill said, 'but every humble home will bless your name if you succeed.'<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>By July 1952 Macmillan was already criticising Butler (then Chancellor of the Exchequer) in his diary, accusing him of "dislik(ing) and fear(ing) him"; in fact there is no evidence that Butler regarded Macmillan as a rival at this stage. In April 1953 Beaverbrook encouraged Macmillan to think that in a future leadership contest he might emerge in a dead heat between Eden and Butler, as the young Beaverbrook (Max Aitken as he had been at the time) had helped <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> to do in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In July 1953 Macmillan considered postponing his gall bladder operation in case Churchill, who had just suffered a serious stroke while Eden was also in hospital, had to step down.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan achieved his housing target by the end of 1953, a year ahead of schedule.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher1982139_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher1982139-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HNDM_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HNDM-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Defence_Minister_(1954–55)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Defence_Minister_.281954.E2.80.9355.29">Defence Minister (1954–55)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Defence Minister (1954–55)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Churchillcabinet1955.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Churchillcabinet1955.png/300px-Churchillcabinet1955.png" width="300" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Churchillcabinet1955.png/450px-Churchillcabinet1955.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/Churchillcabinet1955.png 2x" data-file-width="591" data-file-height="402" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Churchillcabinet1955.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Churchill's Cabinet, 1955 (Macmillan sitting on the far left)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Macmillan was <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Defence" title="Secretary of State for Defence">Minister of Defence</a> from October 1954, but found his authority restricted by Churchill's personal involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher1982143_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher1982143-95">[95]</a></sup> In the opinion of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>: 'He gave the impression that his own undoubted capacity for imaginative running of his own show melted way when an august superior was breathing down his neck.'<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>A major theme of his tenure at Defence was the ministry's growing reliance on the nuclear deterrent, in the view of some critics, to the detriment of conventional forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher1982144.E2.80.935_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher1982144.E2.80.935-97">[97]</a></sup> The Defence White Paper of February 1955, announcing the decision to produce the <a href="/wiki/Teller-Ulam_design" class="mw-redirect" title="Teller-Ulam design">hydrogen bomb</a>, received bipartisan support.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher1982145_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher1982145-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>"It breaks my heart to see the lion-hearted Churchill begin to sink into a sort of <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Petain" class="mw-redirect" title="Philippe Petain">Petain</a>", Macmillan wrote in his diary as the Prime Minister's mental and physical powers visibly decayed. Macmillan was one of the few ministers brave enough to tell Churchill to his face that it was time for him to retire.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Second World War Macmillan's toothy grin, baggy trousers and rimless glasses had given him, as his biographer puts it, "an air of an early Bolshevik leader".<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> By the 1950s he had had his teeth capped, grew his hair in a more shapely style, wore Savile Row suits and walked with the ramrod bearing of a former Guards officer, acquiring the distinguished appearance of his later career.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Campbell writes "there has been no more startling personal reinvention in British politics".<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> He very often wore either an Old Etonian or a Brigade of Guards tie.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Foreign_Secretary_(1955)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_Secretary_.281955.29">Foreign Secretary (1955)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Foreign Secretary (1955)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Macmillan was <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of State for Foreign Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> in April–December 1955 in the government of Anthony Eden, who had taken over as prime minister from the retiring Churchill. Returning from the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Summit_(1955)" title="Geneva Summit (1955)">Geneva Summit</a> of that year he made headlines by declaring: 'There ain't gonna be no war.'<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher1982150_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher1982150-104">[104]</a></sup> Of the role of Foreign Secretary Macmillan observed:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Nothing he can say can do very much good and almost anything he may say may do a great deal of harm. Anything he says that is not obvious is dangerous; whatever is not trite is risky. He is forever poised between the cliché and the indiscretion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFisher1982150_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFisher1982150-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer_(1955–57)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer_.281955.E2.80.9357.29">Chancellor of the Exchequer (1955–57)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chancellor of the Exchequer (1955–57)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Budget">Budget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Budget">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Macmillan was appointed <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> in December 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> He had enjoyed his eight months as Foreign Secretary and did not wish to move. He insisted on being "undisputed head of the home front" and that Eden's de facto deputy <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a>, whom he was replacing as Chancellor, not have the title "Deputy Prime Minister" and not be treated as senior to him. He even tried (in vain) to demand that Salisbury, not Butler, should preside over the Cabinet in Eden's absence. Macmillan later claimed in his memoirs that he had still expected Butler, his junior by eight years, to succeed Eden, but correspondence with <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Marquis,_1st_Earl_of_Woolton" title="Frederick Marquis, 1st Earl of Woolton">Lord Woolton</a> at the time makes clear that Macmillan was very much thinking of the succession. As early as January 1956 he told Eden's press secretary William Clark that it would be "interesting to see how long Anthony can stay in the saddle".<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan planned to reverse the 6d cut in income tax which Butler had made a year previously, but backed off after a "frank talk" with Butler, who threatened resignation, on 28 March 1956. He settled for spending cuts instead, and himself threatened resignation until he was allowed to cut bread and milk subsidies, something the Cabinet had not permitted Butler to do.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of his innovations at the <a href="/wiki/HM_Treasury" title="HM Treasury">Treasury</a> was the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Premium_bonds" class="mw-redirect" title="Premium bonds">premium bonds</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> announced in his budget of 17 April 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2008383_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2008383-109">[109]</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> Opposition initially decried them as a 'squalid raffle', they proved an immediate hit with the public, with £1,000 won in the first prize draw in June 1957.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suez">Suez</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Suez">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In November 1956 Britain invaded <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> in collusion with France and Israel in the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>. According to Labour Shadow Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, Macmillan was 'first in, first out':<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> first very supportive of the invasion, then a prime mover in Britain's humiliating withdrawal in the wake of the financial crisis caused by pressure from the US government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorne2008441_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorne2008441-111">[111]</a></sup> Since the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revolution_of_1952" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian Revolution of 1952">Egyptian Revolution of 1952</a>, relations between Britain and Egypt had deteriorated. The Egyptian government, which came to be dominated by <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, was opposed to the British military presence in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab World">Arab World</a>. The Egyptian nationalisation of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> by Nasser on 26 July 1956 prompted the British government and the French government of <a href="/wiki/Guy_Mollet" title="Guy Mollet">Guy Mollet</a> to commence plans for invading Egypt, regaining the canal, and toppling Nasser. Macmillan wrote in his diary: "If Nasser 'gets away with it', we are done for. The whole Arab world will despise us ... <a href="/wiki/Nuri_al-Said" title="Nuri al-Said">Nuri</a> [es-Said, British-backed Prime Minister of Iraq] and our friends will fall. It may well be the end of British influence and strength forever. So, in the last resort, we must use force and defy opinion, here and overseas".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Macmillan threatened to resign if force was not used against Nasser.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> He was heavily involved in the secret planning of the invasion with France and Israel. It was he who first suggested collusion with Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200674_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200674-114">[114]</a></sup> On 5 August 1956 Macmillan met Churchill at Chartwell, and told him that the government's plan for simply regaining control of the canal was not enough and suggested involving Israel, recording in his diary for that day: "Surely, if we landed we must seek out the Egyptian forces; destroy them; and bring down Nasser's government. Churchill seemed to agree with all this."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Macmillan knew President Eisenhower well, but misjudged his strong opposition to a military solution. Macmillan met Eisenhower privately on 25 September 1956 and convinced himself that the US would not oppose the invasion,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeckett200673.E2.80.934_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeckett200673.E2.80.934-116">[116]</a></sup> despite the misgivings of the British Ambassador, Sir Roger Makins, who was also present. Macmillan failed to heed a warning from Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a> that whatever the British government did should wait until after the US presidential election on 6 November, and failed to report Dulles' remarks to Eden.</p>
<p>The treasury was his portfolio, but he did not recognise the financial disaster that could result from US government actions. Sterling was draining out of the Bank of England at an alarming rate, and it was getting worse. The canal was blocked by the Egyptians, and most oil shipments were delayed as tankers had to go around Africa. The US government refused any financial help until Britain withdrew its forces from Egypt. When he did realise this, he changed his mind and called for withdrawal on US terms, while exaggerating the financial crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-Diane_B._Kunz_1991_pp_130_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diane_B._Kunz_1991_pp_130-117">[117]</a></sup> On 6 November Macmillan informed the Cabinet that Britain had lost $370m in the first few days of November alone.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Faced with Macmillan's prediction of doom, the cabinet had no choice but to accept these terms and withdraw. The Canal remained in Egyptian hands, and Nasser's government continued its support of Arab and African national resistance movements opposed to the British and French presence in the region and on the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-Diane_B._Kunz_1991_pp_130_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diane_B._Kunz_1991_pp_130-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In later life Macmillan was open about his failure to read Eisenhower's thoughts correctly and much regretted the damage done to Anglo-American relations, but always maintained that the Anglo-French military response to the nationalisation of the Canal had been for the best.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> D.R.Thorpe rejects the charge that Macmillan deliberately played false over Suez (i.e. encouraged Eden to attack in order to destroy him as Prime Minister), noting that Macmillan privately put the chances of success at 51–49.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_to_Eden">Succession to Eden</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harold_Macmillan&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Succession to Eden">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Britain's humiliation at the hands of the USA caused deep anger among Conservative MPs. After the ceasefire a motion on the Order Paper attacking the USA for "gravely endangering the Atlantic Alliance" attracted the signatures of over a hundred MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Macmillan tried, but failed, to see Eisenhower (who was also refusing to see Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Selwyn_Lloyd" title="Selwyn Lloyd">Selwyn Lloyd</a>) behind Butler's and Eden's back. Macmillan had a number of meetings with <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Ambassador to the United Kingdom">US Ambassador</a> <a href="/wiki/Winthrop_Aldrich" class="mw-redirect" title="Winthrop Aldrich">Winthrop Aldrich</a>, in which he said that if he were Prime Minister the US Administration would find him much more amenable. Eisenhower encouraged Aldrich to have further meetings. Macmillan and Butler met Aldrich on 21 November. Eisenhower spoke highly of Macmillan ("A straight, fine man, and so far as he is concerned, the outstanding one of the British he served with during the war").<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the evening of 22 November 1956 Butler, who had just announced British withdrawal, addressed the <a href="/wiki/1922_committee" class="mw-redirect" title="1922 committee">1922 committee</a> (Conservative backbenchers) with Macmillan.
